Ash'ari Criterion for Harm and Evil
Nasafi presents the Ash'ari criterion for what constitutes evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari criterion for what makes an act of harm evil, and how this criterion explains the difference between murder and legal retribution.

Core Idea
Harm is not inherently evil; it becomes evil only when it transgresses God's command.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a key Ash'ari principle that distinguishes between harmful acts that are evil and those that are not. According to the Ash'aris, the moral quality of harm depends on whether it conforms to or transgresses the command of the legitimate authority, which is God. This principle is illustrated with a concrete example: the difference between murder and legal retribution (qiṣāṣ).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the principle is stated first, then exemplified.
2
Identify the criterion
Find the condition that makes harm evil: the phrase 'mujāwizan amr āmirih' (transgressing the command of his commander). Understand that the 'commander' ultimately refers to God.
This is the core of the Ash'ari position.
3
Apply the example
Consider the two killings: one intentional murder (evil) and one legal retribution (not evil). Note that the harm to the child is identical, yet the moral judgment differs solely based on the presence or absence of transgression.
This shows that the moral quality is not inherent in the act itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأشعرية يقولون : الإضرار متى لم تكن المضر به غیره مجاوزا آمره لا يكون شرا، ومتى كان مجاوزاً أمر آمره كان شراً، ولهذا يقال: من قتل غيره عمدًا إنه ألحق الشر بولده الصغير ولو قتله بقصاص ما كان شريراً ملحقا الشر بولده الصغير، وإن كان الضرر عليه في الحالين سواء.
Then the Ash'aris say: Harm, when the one harmed by it is not transgressing the command of his commander, is not evil, and when he is transgressing the command of his commander, it is evil. For this reason it is said: He who kills another intentionally, indeed he inflicted evil on his young child, but if he killed him as retribution, he would not be evil, inflicting evil on his young child, even though the harm to him in both cases is the same.
Source pages p. 932

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari principle for defining evil (sharr). According to the Ash'aris, harm is not inherently evil; it becomes evil when it involves transgression against the command of the one in authority. They illustrate with an example: a man who kills another intentionally harms the victim's young child (by depriving them of their father). This is evil because the murderer transgresses God's command. But if the same man kills the same person as legal retaliation (qiṣāṣ), the harm to the child is the same, but the act is not evil because it is commanded by God. Thus, the moral quality depends on whether the act conforms to or transgresses the divine command.

Key Terms
الإضرار
الشر
الأمر
الآمر
القصاص
عدوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harm is always evil regardless of context.
Correction: According to the Ash'aris, harm is only evil when it transgresses the command of the legitimate authority (God). The same harm can be non-evil if it is commanded, as in legal retribution.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented here, when does causing harm become evil?
A) When the harm is severe.
B) When it transgresses the command of the one in authority.
C) When it is done intentionally.
D) When it is not done for punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view holds that harm is not intrinsically evil; it becomes evil only when it transgresses a divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer a loss or harm, what is your immediate inner judgment? Do you instinctively label it as evil? How can this lesson teach you to pause and ask: 'Is this a transgression of God's command, or is it from Him by His wisdom?'
?
Think of a situation where you witnessed harm and felt outrage. How might your judgment change if you applied the Ash'ari criterion? Does it matter whether the harm is a direct consequence of someone's sin or part of God's decree?
?
Are there areas in your life where you rebel against God's commands because you think following them will harm you? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of what is truly harmful and what is truly evil?

The Māturīdī Distinction Between Takwīn and the Created Thing
The author explains why the Māturīdī school does not need to posit aspects of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī rationale for not requiring the theory of aspects (jihāt) of actions, based on the distinction between the act of creation (takwīn) and the created entity.

Core Idea
For the Māturīdīs, the ugliness of the created effect does not make the divine act of creation ugly.

This passage addresses a key theological disagreement within kalām: whether actions have multiple 'aspects' (jihāt) that make them good from one perspective and evil from another. The Māturīdī school offers a different solution based on the distinction between takwīn and the created thing. By understanding their argument, you will see why they consider the jihāt theory unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: takwīn, mukawwan, jihāt, qabīḥ.
Pay attention to the structure: first a general point about necessary knowledge, then the Māturīdī doctrine, then the conclusion about jihāt.
2
Identify the core distinction
Identify the distinction the author makes between 'takwīn' and 'mukawwan'. This is the linchpin of the argument. Understand that kufr is a created thing (mukawwan), but its coming into existence (takwīn) is separate.
3
Trace the logical implication
Follow the logic: if takwīn and the created thing are distinct, then the ugliness of the effect does not transfer to the cause. The act of creation can have a praiseworthy ultimate purpose (ʿāqiba ḥamīda), which overrides the ugliness of the created thing.
This is why they don't need jihāt: they already have a clean separation.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Notice the final sentence: the need for jihāt arises for those who identify takwīn with the created thing. That would force one to say the same action is both good and evil from different aspects. The Māturīdī view avoids that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا وجه لإنكارهم قبح هذه الأشياء كما لا وجه لإنكار حسن كثير من الأجسام؛ لأنه إنكار الضرورة ولو جاز له هذا جاز لغيره إنكار قبح الكفر، وذلك باطل. واعلموا رحمكم الله أن أصحابنا رحمهم الله لما كان من مذهبهم أن التكوين غير المكون والكفر مكون وتكوينه غيره فلم يكن هو عندنا فعل الله تعالى بل هو مفعوله وكون المفعول قبيحًا لا يوجب قبح التكوين إذا كانت فيه عاقبة حميدة، فلم يكن بنا على هذا المذهب حاجة إلى القول بجهات الفعل وأنه بجهة أنه فعل الله تعالى ليس بقبيح وبجهة أنه فعل العبد قبيح، وإنما الحاجة إلى ذلك لمن يزعم أن التكوين هو عين المكون.
There is no justification for their denial of the ugliness of these things, just as there is no justification for denying the goodness of many bodies, because it is a denial of what is known by necessity. If it were permissible for him to do this, it would be permissible for another to deny the ugliness of kufr (disbelief), and that is false. And know, may Allah have mercy on you, that our companions (may Allah have mercy on them), since their doctrine is that takwīn (the act of bringing into existence) is other than the mukawwan (the thing brought into existence), and kufr is a created thing and its creation is other than it, so according to us, it is not an act of Allah the Exalted, but rather it is His effect (maf'ūl). And that the effect is ugly does not necessitate that the creation (takwīn) is ugly, if there is a praiseworthy consequence in it. So on this doctrine, we have no need to say that the action has aspects (jihāt), and that it is not ugly by virtue of being the act of Allah, and ugly by virtue of being the act of the servant. Rather, the need for that is for one who claims that takwīn is identical to the created thing.
Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing the Māturīdī position on why they do not need to resort to the theory of 'aspects of actions' (jihāt al-fiʿl). The author begins by defending the objective reality of good and evil, stating that denying the ugliness of certain things is a denial of necessary knowledge. Then he presents the Māturīdī doctrine: takwīn (divine act of creation) is distinct from the created entity (mukawwan). For example, kufr (disbelief) is a created thing, but its being created (takwīn) is something else. Therefore, kufr, as a created effect, is not an act of Allah in the sense of being His attribute; it is His mafʿūl (effect). The ugliness of the effect does not transfer to the act of creation, especially if there is a praiseworthy ultimate outcome. Consequently, the Māturīdī school does not need to say that an action has multiple aspects (jihāt) such that it is good from one aspect and evil from another. That need arises only for those who conflate takwīn with the created thing (i.e., who deny the distinction between act and effect).

Key Terms
تكوي
مكون
جهات الفعل
قبيح
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that for the Māturīdīs, the ugliness of kufr makes takwīn ugly.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they distinguish between act and effect, so the ugliness of the created thing does not affect the act of creation.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī position in this passage, why is there no need to posit 'aspects' (jihāt) for actions?
A) Because takwīn is identical to the created thing.
B) Because the ugliness of the effect does not entail ugliness of creation, and there is a praiseworthy consequence.
C) Because all actions are good from Allah's aspect.
D) Because the Najjāriyya view is correct.

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdī position avoids the need for the jihāt theory by distinguishing between the divine act of creation (takwīn) and the created effect, so that the ugliness of the effect does not entail the ugliness of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness extreme evil in the world—like oppression or disbelief—does your heart ever quietly question whether God is truly good? How does the Māturīdī distinction between the act of creation and the created thing challenge you to separate God's perfect act from the ugliness of what He creates? Will you now consciously train your heart to see His wisdom, even in the darkest effects?
?
This passage says that the ugliness of the effect does not make the creation ugly if there is a praiseworthy consequence. Think of a personal trial you have faced—something you considered 'ugly' in your life. Can you identify at least one possible praiseworthy outcome that might only be known to Allah? How can you hold onto this truth when you are in the middle of the trial, without waiting for hindsight?
?
The author warns that denying the objective ugliness of evil opens the door to denying even the ugliness of kufr. Have you ever, in an attempt to be 'tolerant,' softened your stance on the reality of sin? How can you maintain a firm conviction that some things are truly evil, without attributing that evil to Allah's act of creation, and without compromising on His perfect goodness? What practical step will you take this week to strengthen this understanding in your heart?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Essential vs Relational Attributes
Nasafi distinguishes attributes that follow from essence from those due to relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between essential attributes (those that follow from the essence) and relational attributes (those that depend on relation), and why this distinction is crucial for preserving God's immutability.

Core Idea
Attributes that depend on relations change without changing God's essence.

This passage from Nasafi tackles a key puzzle in kalam: how can we speak of God as 'Creator' or 'Punisher' without implying that He changes? The answer lies in distinguishing between attributes that belong to God's very essence, which never change, and attributes that are relational, like creating or punishing, which vary according to creation's actions. Nasafi explains this with a logical rule: essential attributes are unchanging because their cause is unchanging, while relational attributes vary because the relation varies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice the parallel structure: 'لا يختلف ما استحقه لذاته' and 'ما كان استحقاقه باختلاف ما يضايقه'. This contrast is the core of the lesson.
2
Identify the two categories
Separate essential attributes (ما استحقه لذاته) from relational attributes (الأوصاف الثابتة باعتبار الإضافة). Note the examples given: for essential, none given here; for relational, examples like obedience/disobedience, wisdom/foolishness, evil/good, and kinship terms.
3
Understand the logical rule
Nasafi states that essential attributes do not differ because their cause does not differ. Relational attributes differ because their cause (the relation) differs. This ensures that God's essence remains unchanged while relational descriptions apply based on creation.
This is a standard Maturidi argument against anthropomorphic change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يختلف ما استحقه لذاته لأن علته لم تختلف فلا يختلف المعلول، وما كان استحقاقه باختلاف ما يضايقه يختلف باختلاف المتضايف، لأن علته تختلف فيختلف المعلول، وما أثبتنا اختلاف الجهات فيه كان من الأوصاف الثابتة باعتبار الإضافة كالطاعة والمعصية والحكمة والسفه والشر والخير وأشباه ذلك، فكان ذلك نظير الأب والابن والعم والخال وأشباه ذلك، والله الموفق.
So what it deserves for its essence does not differ because its cause does not differ, so the effect does not differ. And what deserves [its attribute] due to the difference of what is correlated with it differs with the difference of the correlatives, because its cause differs so the effect differs. And what we have established of differing aspects in it was among the attributes established by way of relation, like obedience and disobedience, wisdom and foolishness, evil and good, and the like. So that became analogous to father, son, uncle, maternal uncle, and the like. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage defines the difference between essential and relational attributes. It first states that an attribute that something deserves by its essence (like existence) does not change because its cause is unchanging. Then it explains that an attribute that depends on a relation (like being a father) varies as the relation varies. The author then gives examples of such relational attributes: obedience/disobedience, wisdom/foolishness, evil/good, etc., and compares them to kinship terms like father and son. The point is that attributing varying descriptions to God (e.g., punishing for unbelief but not for creation) does not imply change in God's essence, but reflects relational aspects.

Key Terms
ما استحقه لذاته
ما يضايقه
الأوصاف الثابتة باعتبار الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributes like 'creator' change God, implying that God changes when He creates.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that relational attributes like 'creator' depend on the relation to creation, not on God's essence. They vary without causing any change in God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what type of attributes vary with a change in relation?
A) Essential attributes
B) Relational attributes
C) Both essential and relational
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are unchanging because they arise from God's essence; relational attributes vary with creation without implying change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a divine name like 'The Avenger' (Al-Muntaqim), do you struggle with how a God of mercy also punishes? How does today's lesson help you worship without doubting His unchanging mercy? What specific change in your du'a will you make tonight to address this?
?
Think of a time you felt God was 'distant' because your circumstances were hard. Did you implicitly assume God's attribute of 'closeness' had changed? Knowing that relational attributes shift without altering His essence, how can you now anchor your trust in His essential mercy even when you don't feel it? Write down one verse or name that reminds you of His unchanging essence.
?
This lesson compares relational attributes to kinship terms like 'father' and 'son'. How does this analogy humble you when you are tempted to take pride in your obedience or despair over your sin? Does your obedience make God 'more' pleased in His essence? If not, how should this reshape your motivation for worship tonight?
